			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Mathematics</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about the study of topics such as quantity and structure. For other uses, see <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Mathematics (disambiguation)">Mathematics (disambiguation)</a>. "Math" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Math_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Math (disambiguation)">Math (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Euclid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="./normalization_files/220px-Euclid.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Euclid.jpg/330px-Euclid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Euclid.jpg/440px-Euclid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="806" data-file-height="675"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Euclid.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> (holding <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calipers" title="Calipers">calipers</a>), Greek mathematician, 3rd century BC, as imagined by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> in this detail from <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">The School of Athens</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Mathematics</b> (from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>  <i>mthma</i>, knowledge, study, learning) is the study of topics such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantity</a> (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number" title="Number">numbers</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-OED_2-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-OED-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_structure" title="Mathematical structure">structure</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Kneebone_3-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Kneebone-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-OED_2-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-OED-2">[2]</a></sup> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LaTorre_4-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-LaTorre-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ramana_5-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Ramana-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ziegler_6-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Ziegler-6">[6]</a></sup> There is a range of views among mathematicians and philosophers as to the exact scope and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Definitions_of_mathematics" title="Definitions of mathematics">definition of mathematics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mura_7-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Mura-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Runge_8-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Runge-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">Mathematicians</a> seek out <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patterns" class="mw-redirect" title="Patterns">patterns</a><sup id="cite_ref-future_9-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-future-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-devlin_10-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-devlin-10">[10]</a></sup> and use them to formulate new <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjectures</a>. Mathematicians resolve the truth or falsity of conjectures by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_proof" title="Mathematical proof">mathematical proof</a>. When mathematical structures are good models of real phenomena, then mathematical reasoning can provide insight or predictions about nature. Through the use of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstraction_(mathematics)" title="Abstraction (mathematics)">abstraction</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, mathematics developed from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counting" title="Counting">counting</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculation" title="Calculation">calculation</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurement</a>, and the systematic study of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shape" title="Shape">shapes</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Motion_(physics)" title="Motion (physics)">motions</a> of physical objects. Practical mathematics has been a human activity from as far back as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Mathematics" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Mathematics">written records</a> exist. The research required to solve mathematical problems can take years or even centuries of sustained inquiry.</p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Rigorous arguments</a> first appeared in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">Greek mathematics</a>, most notably in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>'s <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid&#39;s Elements">Elements</a></i>. Since the pioneering work of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giuseppe_Peano" title="Giuseppe Peano">Giuseppe Peano</a> (18581932), <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a> (18621943), and others <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">on axiomatic systems in the late 19th century</a>, it has become customary to view mathematical research as establishing <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_rigour" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematical rigour">rigorous</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduction</a> from appropriately chosen <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Definition" title="Definition">definitions</a>. Mathematics developed at a relatively slow pace until the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, when mathematical innovations interacting with new <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries" title="Timeline of scientific discoveries">scientific discoveries</a> led to a rapid increase in the rate of mathematical discovery that has continued to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> (15641642) said, "The universe cannot be read until we have learned the language and become familiar with the characters in which it is written. It is written in mathematical language, and the letters are triangles, circles and other geometrical figures, without which means it is humanly impossible to comprehend a single word. Without these, one is wandering about in a dark labyrinth."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> (17771855) referred to mathematics as "the Queen of the Sciences".<sup id="cite_ref-Waltershausen_13-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Waltershausen-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Peirce" title="Benjamin Peirce">Benjamin Peirce</a> (18091880) called mathematics "the science that draws necessary conclusions".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> David Hilbert said of mathematics: "We are not speaking here of arbitrariness in any sense. Mathematics is not like a game whose tasks are determined by arbitrarily stipulated rules. Rather, it is a conceptual system possessing internal necessity that can only be so and by no means otherwise."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> (18791955) stated that "as far as the laws of mathematics refer to reality, they are not certain; and as far as they are certain, they do not refer to reality."<sup id="cite_ref-certain_16-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-certain-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mathematics is essential in many fields, including <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finance" title="Finance">finance</a> and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a>. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">Applied mathematics</a> has led to entirely new mathematical disciplines, such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a>. Mathematicians also engage in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pure_mathematics" title="Pure mathematics">pure mathematics</a>, or mathematics for its own sake, without having any application in mind. There is no clear line separating pure and applied mathematics, and practical applications for what began as pure mathematics are often discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=1&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: History">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_mathematics" title="History of mathematics">History of mathematics</a></div>
<p>The history of mathematics can be seen as an ever-increasing series of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstraction_(mathematics)" title="Abstraction (mathematics)">abstractions</a>. The first abstraction, which is shared by many animals,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> was probably that of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number" title="Number">numbers</a>: the realization that a collection of two apples and a collection of two oranges (for example) have something in common, namely quantity of their members.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="./normalization_files/170px-Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg/255px-Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg/340px-Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg 2x" data-file-width="449" data-file-height="599"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Greek mathematician <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> (<span class="nowrap">c. 570  c. 495 BC</span>), commonly credited with discovering the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Maya.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="./normalization_files/170px-Maya.svg.png" width="170" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Maya.svg/255px-Maya.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Maya.svg/340px-Maya.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="248" data-file-height="286"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Maya.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mayan_numerals" class="mw-redirect" title="Mayan numerals">Mayan numerals</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As evidenced by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tally_sticks" class="mw-redirect" title="Tally sticks">tallies</a> found on bone, in addition to recognizing how to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counting" title="Counting">count</a> physical objects, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prehistoric" class="mw-redirect" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a> peoples may have also recognized how to count abstract quantities, like time&nbsp; days, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Season" title="Season">seasons</a>, years.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Evidence for more complex mathematics does not appear until around 3000&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Before_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Before Christ">BC</a>, when the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a> and Egyptians began using arithmetic, algebra and geometry for <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxation" class="mw-redirect" title="Taxation">taxation</a> and other financial calculations, for building and construction, and for <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The earliest uses of mathematics were in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trading</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_measurement" class="mw-redirect" title="Land measurement">land measurement</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Painting" title="Painting">painting</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a> patterns and the recording of time.</p>
<p>In <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonian_mathematics" title="Babylonian mathematics">Babylonian mathematics</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elementary_arithmetic" title="Elementary arithmetic">elementary arithmetic</a> (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Addition" title="Addition">addition</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subtraction" title="Subtraction">subtraction</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiplication" title="Multiplication">multiplication</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Division_(mathematics)" title="Division (mathematics)">division</a>) first appears in the archaeological record. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numeracy" title="Numeracy">Numeracy</a> pre-dated <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numeral_system" title="Numeral system">numeral systems</a> have been many and diverse, with the first known written numerals created by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a> in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a> texts such as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus" title="Rhind Mathematical Papyrus">Rhind Mathematical Papyrus</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Between 600 and 300&nbsp;BC the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greeks">Ancient Greeks</a> began a systematic study of mathematics in its own right with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">Greek mathematics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Persian_Khwarazmi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="./normalization_files/170px-Persian_Khwarazmi.jpg" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Persian_Khwarazmi.jpg/255px-Persian_Khwarazmi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/Persian_Khwarazmi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="299" data-file-height="380"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Persian_Khwarazmi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Persian mathematician <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi" title="Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi">Al-Khwarizmi</a> ( c.780c.850 ), the inventor of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">Algebra</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Golden Age of Islam</a>, especially during the 9th and 10th centuries, mathematics saw many important innovations building on Greek mathematics: most of them include the contributions from Persian mathematicians such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi" title="Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi">Al-Khwarismi</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omar_Khayyam" title="Omar Khayyam">Omar Khayyam</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sharaf_al-D%C4%ABn_al-%E1%B9%AC%C5%ABs%C4%AB" title="Sharaf al-Dn al-s">Sharaf al-Dn al-s</a>.</p>
<p>Mathematics has since been greatly extended, and there has been a fruitful interaction between mathematics and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, to the benefit of both. Mathematical discoveries continue to be made today. According to Mikhail B. Sevryuk, in the January 2006 issue of the <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulletin_of_the_American_Mathematical_Society" title="Bulletin of the American Mathematical Society">Bulletin of the American Mathematical Society</a></i>, "The number of papers and books included in the <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_Reviews" title="Mathematical Reviews">Mathematical Reviews</a></i> database since 1940 (the first year of operation of MR) is now more than 1.9&nbsp;million, and more than 75&nbsp;thousand items are added to the database each year. The overwhelming majority of works in this ocean contain new mathematical <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theorem" title="Theorem">theorems</a> and their <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_proof" title="Mathematical proof">proofs</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESevryuk2006101.E2.80.9309_22-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-FOOTNOTESevryuk2006101.E2.80.9309-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=2&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word <i>mathematics</i> comes from the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>  (<i>mthma</i>), which, in the ancient Greek language, means "that which is learnt",<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> "what one gets to know", hence also "study" and "science", and in modern Greek just "lesson". The word <i>mthma</i> is derived from  (<i>manthano</i>), while the modern Greek equivalent is  (<i>mathaino</i>), both of which mean "to learn". In Greece, the word for "mathematics" came to have the narrower and more technical meaning "mathematical study" even in Classical times.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Its adjective is <span lang="grc"></span> (<i>mathmatiks</i>), meaning "related to learning" or "studious", which likewise further came to mean "mathematical". In particular, <span lang="grc"> </span> (<i>mathmatik tkhn</i>), <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la"><i>ars mathematica</i></span>, meant "the mathematical art".</p>
<p>Similarly, one of the two main schools of thought in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pythagoreanism" title="Pythagoreanism">Pythagoreanism</a> was known as the <i>mathmatikoi</i> ()  which at the time meant "teachers" rather than "mathematicians" in the modern sense.</p>
<p>In Latin, and in English until around 1700, the term <i>mathematics</i> more commonly meant "astrology" (or sometimes "astronomy") rather than "mathematics"; the meaning gradually changed to its present one from about 1500 to 1800. This has resulted in several mistranslations: a particularly notorious one is <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saint_Augustine" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Augustine">Saint Augustine</a>'s warning that Christians should beware of <i>mathematici</i> meaning astrologers, which is sometimes mistranslated as a condemnation of mathematicians.<sup id="cite_ref-ohiostateuniversity_25-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-ohiostateuniversity-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The apparent plural form in English, like the French plural form <span lang="fr"><i>les mathmatiques</i></span> (and the less commonly used singular derivative <span lang="fr"><i>la mathmatique</i></span>), goes back to the Latin neuter plural <span lang="la"><i>mathematica</i></span> (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>), based on the Greek plural <span lang="el"> </span> (<i>ta mathmatik</i>), used by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384322&nbsp;BC), and meaning roughly "all things mathematical"; although it is plausible that English borrowed only the adjective <i>mathematic(al)</i> and formed the noun <i>mathematics</i> anew, after the pattern of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, which were inherited from the Greek.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In English, the noun <i>mathematics</i> takes singular verb forms. It is often shortened to <i>maths</i> or, in English-speaking North America, <i>math</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-maths_27-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-maths-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_of_mathematics">Definitions of mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=3&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Definitions of mathematics">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Definitions_of_mathematics" title="Definitions of mathematics">Definitions of mathematics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Fibonacci.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="./normalization_files/170px-Fibonacci.jpg" width="170" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Fibonacci.jpg/255px-Fibonacci.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Fibonacci.jpg/340px-Fibonacci.jpg 2x" data-file-width="356" data-file-height="480"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Fibonacci.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonardo_Fibonacci" class="mw-redirect" title="Leonardo Fibonacci">Leonardo Fibonacci</a>, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italian</a> mathematician who introduced the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="HinduArabic numeral system">HinduArabic numeral system</a> to the Western World</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> defined mathematics as "the science of quantity", and this definition prevailed until the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Franklin_28-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Franklin-28">[28]</a></sup> Starting in the 19th&nbsp;century, when the study of mathematics increased in rigor and began to address abstract topics such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_theory" title="Group theory">group theory</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a>, which have no clear-cut relation to quantity and measurement, mathematicians and philosophers began to propose a variety of new definitions.<sup id="cite_ref-Cajori_29-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Cajori-29">[29]</a></sup> Some of these definitions emphasize the deductive character of much of mathematics, some emphasize its abstractness, some emphasize certain topics within mathematics. Today, no consensus on the definition of mathematics prevails, even among professionals.<sup id="cite_ref-Mura_7-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Mura-7">[7]</a></sup> There is not even consensus on whether mathematics is an art or a science.<sup id="cite_ref-Runge_8-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Runge-8">[8]</a></sup> A great many professional mathematicians take no interest in a definition of mathematics, or consider it undefinable.<sup id="cite_ref-Mura_7-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Mura-7">[7]</a></sup> Some just say, "Mathematics is what mathematicians do."<sup id="cite_ref-Mura_7-3" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Mura-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three leading types of definition of mathematics are called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logicist" class="mw-redirect" title="Logicist">logicist</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intuitionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Intuitionist">intuitionist</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formalism_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Formalism (mathematics)">formalist</a>, each reflecting a different philosophical school of thought.<sup id="cite_ref-Snapper_30-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Snapper-30">[30]</a></sup> All have severe problems, none has widespread acceptance, and no reconciliation seems possible.<sup id="cite_ref-Snapper_30-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Snapper-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>An early definition of mathematics in terms of logic was <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Peirce" title="Benjamin Peirce">Benjamin Peirce</a>'s "the science that draws necessary conclusions" (1870).<sup id="cite_ref-Peirce_31-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Peirce-31">[31]</a></sup> In the <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a> advanced the philosophical program known as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logicism" title="Logicism">logicism</a>, and attempted to prove that all mathematical concepts, statements, and principles can be defined and proved entirely in terms of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolic_logic" class="mw-redirect" title="Symbolic logic">symbolic logic</a>. A logicist definition of mathematics is Russell's "All Mathematics is Symbolic Logic" (1903).<sup id="cite_ref-Russell_32-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Russell-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intuitionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Intuitionist">Intuitionist</a> definitions, developing from the philosophy of mathematician <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L.E.J._Brouwer" class="mw-redirect" title="L.E.J. Brouwer">L.E.J. Brouwer</a>, identify mathematics with certain mental phenomena. An example of an intuitionist definition is "Mathematics is the mental activity which consists in carrying out constructs one after the other."<sup id="cite_ref-Snapper_30-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Snapper-30">[30]</a></sup> A peculiarity of intuitionism is that it rejects some mathematical ideas considered valid according to other definitions. In particular, while other philosophies of mathematics allow objects that can be proved to exist even though they cannot be constructed, intuitionism allows only mathematical objects that one can actually construct.</p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formalism_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Formalism (mathematics)">Formalist</a> definitions identify mathematics with its symbols and the rules for operating on them. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haskell_Curry" title="Haskell Curry">Haskell Curry</a> defined mathematics simply as "the science of formal systems".<sup id="cite_ref-Curry_33-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Curry-33">[33]</a></sup> A <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_system" title="Formal system">formal system</a> is a set of symbols, or <i>tokens</i>, and some <i>rules</i> telling how the tokens may be combined into <i>formulas</i>. In formal systems, the word <i>axiom</i> has a special meaning, different from the ordinary meaning of "a self-evident truth". In formal systems, an axiom is a combination of tokens that is included in a given formal system without needing to be derived using the rules of the system.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics_as_science">Mathematics as science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=4&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Mathematics as science">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="./normalization_files/170px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg/255px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg/340px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="738"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>, known as the prince of mathematicians</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Gauss</a> referred to mathematics as "the Queen of the Sciences".<sup id="cite_ref-Waltershausen_13-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-Waltershausen-13">[13]</a></sup> In the original Latin <i>Regina Scientiarum</i>, as well as in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Knigin der Wissenschaften</i>, the word corresponding to <i>science</i> means a "field of knowledge", and this was the original meaning of "science" in English, also; mathematics is in this sense a field of knowledge. The specialization restricting the meaning of "science" to <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a></i> follows the rise of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baconian_method" title="Baconian method">Baconian science</a>, which contrasted "natural science" to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a>, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organon" title="Organon">Aristotelean method</a> of inquiring from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_principles" class="mw-redirect" title="First principles">first principles</a>. The role of empirical experimentation and observation is negligible in mathematics, compared to natural sciences such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> stated that "as far as the laws of mathematics refer to reality, they are not certain; and as far as they are certain, they do not refer to reality."<sup id="cite_ref-certain_16-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-certain-16">[16]</a></sup> More recently, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marcus_du_Sautoy" title="Marcus du Sautoy">Marcus du Sautoy</a> has called mathematics "the Queen of Science&nbsp;... the main driving force behind scientific discovery".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many philosophers believe that mathematics is not experimentally <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">falsifiable</a>, and thus not a science according to the definition of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> However, in the 1930s <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorems" title="Gdel&#39;s incompleteness theorems">Gdel's incompleteness theorems</a> convinced many mathematicians<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2011)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> that mathematics cannot be reduced to logic alone, and Karl Popper concluded that "most mathematical theories are, like those of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothetico</a>-<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deductive" class="mw-redirect" title="Deductive">deductive</a>: pure mathematics therefore turns out to be much closer to the natural sciences whose hypotheses are conjectures, than it seemed even recently."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Other thinkers, notably <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imre_Lakatos" title="Imre Lakatos">Imre Lakatos</a>, have applied a version of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falsificationism" class="mw-redirect" title="Falsificationism">falsificationism</a> to mathematics itself.</p>
<p>An alternative view is that certain scientific fields (such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a>) are mathematics with axioms that are intended to correspond to reality. The theoretical physicist <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J.M._Ziman" class="mw-redirect" title="J.M. Ziman">J.M. Ziman</a> proposed that science is <i>public knowledge</i>, and thus includes mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Mathematics shares much in common with many fields in the physical sciences, notably the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">exploration of the logical consequences</a> of assumptions. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intuition_(knowledge)" class="mw-redirect" title="Intuition (knowledge)">Intuition</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experimentation</a> also play a role in the formulation of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjectures</a> in both mathematics and the (other) sciences. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Experimental_mathematics" title="Experimental mathematics">Experimental mathematics</a> continues to grow in importance within mathematics, and computation and simulation are playing an increasing role in both the sciences and mathematics.</p>
<p>The opinions of mathematicians on this matter are varied. Many mathematicians<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2009)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> feel that to call their area a science is to downplay the importance of its aesthetic side, and its history in the traditional seven <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberal_arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal arts">liberal arts</a>; others<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2009)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> feel that to ignore its connection to the sciences is to turn a blind eye to the fact that the interface between mathematics and its applications in science and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> has driven much development in mathematics. One way this difference of viewpoint plays out is in the philosophical debate as to whether mathematics is <i>created</i> (as in art) or <i>discovered</i> (as in science). It is common to see <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University" title="University">universities</a> divided into sections that include a division of <i>Science and Mathematics</i>, indicating that the fields are seen as being allied but that they do not coincide. In practice, mathematicians are typically grouped with scientists at the gross level but separated at finer levels. This is one of many issues considered in the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">philosophy of mathematics</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inspiration.2C_pure_and_applied_mathematics.2C_and_aesthetics">Inspiration, pure and applied mathematics, and aesthetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=5&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Inspiration, pure and applied mathematics, and aesthetics">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_beauty" title="Mathematical beauty">Mathematical beauty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;max-width:322px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:153px;max-width:153px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:206px;overflow:hidden"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Isaac Newton" src="./normalization_files/151px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" width="151" height="207" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/227px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/302px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 2x" data-file-width="407" data-file-height="559"></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:165px;max-width:165px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:206px;overflow:hidden"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gottfried_Wilhelm_von_Leibniz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Gottfried Wilhelm von Leibniz" src="./normalization_files/163px-Gottfried_Wilhelm_von_Leibniz.jpg" width="163" height="206" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Gottfried_Wilhelm_von_Leibniz.jpg/245px-Gottfried_Wilhelm_von_Leibniz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/Gottfried_Wilhelm_von_Leibniz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="316" data-file-height="400"></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (left) and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz</a> (right), developers of infinitesimal calculus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mathematics arises from many different kinds of problems. At first these were found in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_measurement" class="mw-redirect" title="Land measurement">land measurement</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a> and later <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>; today, all sciences suggest problems studied by mathematicians, and many problems arise within mathematics itself. For example, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Richard Feynman</a> invented the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Path_integral_formulation" title="Path integral formulation">path integral formulation</a> of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> using a combination of mathematical reasoning and physical insight, and today's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>, a still-developing scientific theory which attempts to unify the four <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental forces of nature</a>, continues to inspire new mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some mathematics is relevant only in the area that inspired it, and is applied to solve further problems in that area. But often mathematics inspired by one area proves useful in many areas, and joins the general stock of mathematical concepts. A distinction is often made between <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pure_mathematics" title="Pure mathematics">pure mathematics</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">applied mathematics</a>. However pure mathematics topics often turn out to have applications, e.g. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a> in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptography</a>. This remarkable fact, that even the "purest" mathematics often turns out to have practical applications, is what <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eugene_Wigner" title="Eugene Wigner">Eugene Wigner</a> has called "<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Unreasonable_Effectiveness_of_Mathematics_in_the_Natural_Sciences" title="The Unreasonable Effectiveness of Mathematics in the Natural Sciences">the unreasonable effectiveness of mathematics</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> As in most areas of study, the explosion of knowledge in the scientific age has led to specialization: there are now hundreds of specialized areas in mathematics and the latest <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics_Subject_Classification" title="Mathematics Subject Classification">Mathematics Subject Classification</a> runs to 46&nbsp;pages.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Several areas of applied mathematics have merged with related traditions outside of mathematics and become disciplines in their own right, including <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
<p>For those who are mathematically inclined, there is often a definite aesthetic aspect to much of mathematics. Many mathematicians talk about the <i>elegance</i> of mathematics, its intrinsic <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a> and inner <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beauty" title="Beauty">beauty</a>. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simplicity" title="Simplicity">Simplicity</a> and generality are valued. There is beauty in a simple and elegant <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Proof (mathematics)">proof</a>, such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>'s proof that there are infinitely many <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a>, and in an elegant <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numerical_method" title="Numerical method">numerical method</a> that speeds calculation, such as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fast_Fourier_transform" title="Fast Fourier transform">fast Fourier transform</a>. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G.H._Hardy" class="mw-redirect" title="G.H. Hardy">G.H. Hardy</a> in <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Mathematician%27s_Apology" title="A Mathematician&#39;s Apology">A Mathematician's Apology</a></i> expressed the belief that these aesthetic considerations are, in themselves, sufficient to justify the study of pure mathematics. He identified criteria such as significance, unexpectedness, inevitability, and economy as factors that contribute to a mathematical aesthetic.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Mathematicians often strive to find proofs that are particularly elegant, proofs from "The Book" of God according to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Erd%C5%91s" title="Paul Erds">Paul Erds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The popularity of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recreational_mathematics" title="Recreational mathematics">recreational mathematics</a> is another sign of the pleasure many find in solving mathematical questions.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notation.2C_language.2C_and_rigor">Notation, language, and rigor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=6&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Notation, language, and rigor">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_notation" title="Mathematical notation">Mathematical notation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Leonhard_Euler_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="./normalization_files/170px-Leonhard_Euler_2.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Leonhard_Euler_2.jpg/255px-Leonhard_Euler_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Leonhard_Euler_2.jpg/340px-Leonhard_Euler_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="614" data-file-height="767"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Leonhard_Euler_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a>, who created and popularized much of the mathematical notation used today</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of the mathematical notation in use today was not invented until the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Before that, mathematics was written out in words, limiting mathematical discovery.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Euler</a> (17071783) was responsible for many of the notations in use today. Modern notation makes mathematics much easier for the professional, but beginners often find it daunting. It is compressed: a few symbols contain a great deal of information. Like <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musical_notation" title="Musical notation">musical notation</a>, modern mathematical notation has a strict syntax and encodes information that would be difficult to write in any other way.</p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Language_of_mathematics" title="Language of mathematics">Mathematical language</a> can be difficult to understand for beginners. Common words such as <i>or</i> and <i>only</i> have more precise meanings than in everyday speech. Moreover, words such as <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open_set" title="Open set">open</a></i> and <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">field</a></i> have specialized mathematical meanings. Technical terms such as <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homeomorphism" title="Homeomorphism">homeomorphism</a></i> and <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">integrable</a></i> have precise meanings in mathematics. Additionally, shorthand phrases such as <i>iff</i> for "<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a>" belong to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_jargon" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematical jargon">mathematical jargon</a>. There is a reason for special notation and technical vocabulary: mathematics requires more precision than everyday speech. Mathematicians refer to this precision of language and logic as "rigor".</p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_proof" title="Mathematical proof">Mathematical proof</a> is fundamentally a matter of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rigor" class="mw-redirect" title="Rigor">rigor</a>. Mathematicians want their theorems to follow from axioms by means of systematic reasoning. This is to avoid mistaken "<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theorem" title="Theorem">theorems</a>", based on fallible intuitions, of which many instances have occurred in the history of the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The level of rigor expected in mathematics has varied over time: the Greeks expected detailed arguments, but at the time of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> the methods employed were less rigorous. Problems inherent in the definitions used by Newton would lead to a resurgence of careful analysis and formal proof in the 19th&nbsp;century. Misunderstanding the rigor is a cause for some of the common misconceptions of mathematics. Today, mathematicians continue to argue among themselves about <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer-assisted_proof" title="Computer-assisted proof">computer-assisted proofs</a>. Since large computations are hard to verify, such proofs may not be sufficiently rigorous.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">Axioms</a> in traditional thought were "self-evident truths", but that conception is problematic.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> At a formal level, an axiom is just a string of symbols, which has an intrinsic meaning only in the context of all derivable formulas of an <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiomatic_system" title="Axiomatic system">axiomatic system</a>. It was the goal of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_program" title="Hilbert&#39;s program">Hilbert's program</a> to put all of mathematics on a firm axiomatic basis, but according to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Gdel&#39;s incompleteness theorem">Gdel's incompleteness theorem</a> every (sufficiently powerful) axiomatic system has <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independence_(mathematical_logic)" title="Independence (mathematical logic)">undecidable</a> formulas; and so a final <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiomatization" class="mw-redirect" title="Axiomatization">axiomatization</a> of mathematics is impossible. Nonetheless mathematics is often imagined to be (as far as its formal content) nothing but <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a> in some axiomatization, in the sense that every mathematical statement or proof could be cast into formulas within set theory.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fields_of_mathematics">Fields of mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=7&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Fields of mathematics">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Areas_of_mathematics" title="Areas of mathematics">Areas of mathematics</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_areas_of_mathematics" title="Glossary of areas of mathematics">Glossary of areas of mathematics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Abacus_6.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="./normalization_files/220px-Abacus_6.png" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Abacus_6.png 1.5x" data-file-width="247" data-file-height="145"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Abacus_6.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abacus" title="Abacus">abacus</a>, a simple calculating tool used since ancient times</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mathematics can, broadly speaking, be subdivided into the study of quantity, structure, space, and change (i.e. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis" title="Mathematical analysis">analysis</a>). In addition to these main concerns, there are also subdivisions dedicated to exploring links from the heart of mathematics to other fields: to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">logic</a>, to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a> (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations</a>), to the empirical mathematics of the various sciences (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">applied mathematics</a>), and more recently to the rigorous study of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uncertainty" title="Uncertainty">uncertainty</a>. While some areas might seem unrelated, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Langlands_program" title="Langlands program">Langlands program</a> has found connections between areas previously thought unconnected, such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galois_groups" class="mw-redirect" title="Galois groups">Galois groups</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_surface" title="Riemann surface">Riemann surfaces</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundations_and_philosophy">Foundations and philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=8&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Foundations and philosophy">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In order to clarify the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a>, the fields of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a> were developed. Mathematical logic includes the mathematical study of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> and the applications of formal logic to other areas of mathematics; set theory is the branch of mathematics that studies <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">sets</a> or collections of objects. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Category_theory" title="Category theory">Category theory</a>, which deals in an abstract way with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_structure" title="Mathematical structure">mathematical structures</a> and relationships between them, is still in development. The phrase "crisis of foundations" describes the search for a rigorous foundation for mathematics that took place from approximately 1900 to 1930.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Some disagreement about the foundations of mathematics continues to the present day. The crisis of foundations was stimulated by a number of controversies at the time, including the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Controversy_over_Cantor%27s_theory" title="Controversy over Cantor&#39;s theory">controversy over Cantor's set theory</a> and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brouwer%E2%80%93Hilbert_controversy" title="BrouwerHilbert controversy">BrouwerHilbert controversy</a>.</p>
<p>Mathematical logic is concerned with setting mathematics within a rigorous <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axiomatic</a> framework, and studying the implications of such a framework. As such, it is home to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorems" title="Gdel&#39;s incompleteness theorems">Gdel's incompleteness theorems</a> which (informally) imply that any effective <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_system" title="Formal system">formal system</a> that contains basic arithmetic, if <i>sound</i> (meaning that all theorems that can be proved are true), is necessarily <i>incomplete</i> (meaning that there are true theorems which cannot be proved <i>in that system</i>). Whatever finite collection of number-theoretical axioms is taken as a foundation, Gdel showed how to construct a formal statement that is a true number-theoretical fact, but which does not follow from those axioms. Therefore, no formal system is a complete axiomatization of full number theory. Modern logic is divided into <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recursion_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Recursion theory">recursion theory</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Model_theory" title="Model theory">model theory</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_theory" title="Proof theory">proof theory</a>, and is closely linked to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">theoretical computer science</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> as well as to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Category_theory" title="Category theory">category theory</a>. In the context of recursion theory, the impossibility of a full axiomatization of number theory can also be formally demonstrated as a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MRDP_theorem#Further_applications" class="mw-redirect" title="MRDP theorem">consequence of the MRDP theorem</a>.</p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">Theoretical computer science</a> includes <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computability_theory_(computation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Computability theory (computation)">computability theory</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computational_complexity_theory" title="Computational complexity theory">computational complexity theory</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>. Computability theory examines the limitations of various theoretical models of the computer, including the most well-known model&nbsp; the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turing_machine" title="Turing machine">Turing machine</a>. Complexity theory is the study of tractability by computer; some problems, although theoretically solvable by computer, are so expensive in terms of time or space that solving them is likely to remain practically unfeasible, even with the rapid advancement of computer hardware. A famous problem is the "<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P_%3D_NP_problem" class="mw-redirect" title="P = NP problem"><span class="nowrap"><b>P</b> = <b>NP</b>?</span></a>" problem, one of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millennium_Prize_Problems" title="Millennium Prize Problems">Millennium Prize Problems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics?oldformat=true#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Finally, information theory is concerned with the amount of data that can be stored on a given medium, and hence deals with concepts such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_compression" title="Data compression">compression</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entropy_(information_theory)" title="Entropy (information theory)">entropy</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pure_mathematics">Pure mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=9&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Pure mathematics">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantity">Quantity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=10&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Quantity">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The study of quantity starts with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number" title="Number">numbers</a>, first the familiar <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural numbers</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a> ("whole numbers") and arithmetical operations on them, which are characterized in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a>. The deeper properties of integers are studied in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a>, from which come such popular results as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat%27s_Last_Theorem" title="Fermat&#39;s Last Theorem">Fermat's Last Theorem</a>. The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twin_prime" title="Twin prime">twin prime</a> conjecture and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldbach%27s_conjecture" title="Goldbach&#39;s conjecture">Goldbach's conjecture</a> are two unsolved problems in number theory.</p>
<p>As the number system is further developed, the integers are recognized as a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subset" title="Subset">subset</a> of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational numbers</a> ("<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fraction_(mathematics)" title="Fraction (mathematics)">fractions</a>"). These, in turn, are contained within the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a>, which are used to represent <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_function" title="Continuous function">continuous</a> quantities. Real numbers are generalized to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a>. These are the first steps of a hierarchy of numbers that goes on to include <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quaternion" title="Quaternion">quaternions</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octonion" title="Octonion">octonions</a>. Consideration of the natural numbers also leads to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transfinite_number" title="Transfinite number">transfinite numbers</a>, which formalize the concept of "<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinity" title="Infinity">infinity</a>". According to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra" title="Fundamental theorem of algebra">fundamental theorem of algebra</a> all solutions of equations in one unknown with complex coefficients are complex numbers, regardless of degree. Another area of study is the size of sets, which is described with the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardinal_number" title="Cardinal number">cardinal numbers</a>. These include the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleph_number" title="Aleph number">aleph numbers</a>, which allow meaningful comparison of the size of infinitely large sets.</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mathematics&amp;section=11&amp;veaction=editsource" title="Edit section: Structure">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many mathematical objects, such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">sets</a> of numbers and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function (mathematics)">functions</a>, exhibit internal structure as a consequence of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_(mathematics)" title="Operation (mathematics)">operations</a> or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relation_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Relation (mathematics)">relations</a> that are defined on the set. Mathematics then studies properties of those sets that can be expressed in terms of that structure; for instance <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a> studies properties of the set of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a> that can be expressed in terms of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a> operations. Moreover, it frequently happens that different such structured sets (or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_structure" title="Mathematical structure">structures</a>) exhibit similar properties, which makes it possible, by a further step of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstraction" title="Abstraction">abstraction</a>, to state <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a> for a class of structures, and then study at once the whole class of structures satisfying these axioms. Thus one can study <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_(mathematics)" title="Group (mathematics)">groups</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">rings</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">fields</a> and other abstract systems; together such studies (for structures defined by algebraic operations) constitute the domain of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a>.</p>
<p>By its great generality, abstract algebra can often be applied to seemingly unrelated problems; for instance a number of ancient problems concerning <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions" class="mw-redirect" title="Compass and straightedge constructions">compass and straightedge constructions</a> were finally solved using <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galois_theory" title="Galois theory">Galois theory</a>, which involves field theory and group theory. Another example of an algebraic theory is <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_algebra" title="Linear algebra">linear algebra</a>, which is the general study of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vector spaces</a>, whose elements called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_(geometric)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vector (geometric)">vectors</a> have both quantity and direction, and can be used to model (relations between) points in space. This is one example of the phenomenon that the originally unrelated areas of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a> have very strong interactions in modern mathematics. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Combinatorics" title="Combinatorics">Combinatorics</a> studies ways of enumerating the number of objects that fit a given structure.</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<table style="border:1px solid #ddd; text-align:center; margin:auto" cellspacing="15">

  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mtable rowspacing="4pt" columnspacing="1em">
